<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Franco-Ontarian" title="Franco-Ontarian">Franco-Ontarian</a></div>
<p>The principal language of Ontario is <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, the province's <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> official language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> which is spoken natively by about 70% of the province's population, according to the 2011 census. There is also a <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>-speaking population concentrated in the northeastern, eastern, and extreme Southern parts of the province, where under the <a href="/wiki/French_Language_Services_Act" title="French Language Services Act">French Language Services Act</a>, provincial government services are required to be available in French if at least 10% of a designated area's population report French as their native language. Roughly 4% of Ontarians speak French as their mother tongue,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-46">[46]</a></sup> and 11% are bilingual, speaking both English and French, according to the 2011 census.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-46">[46]</a></sup> Other languages spoken by residents include Arabic, Bengali, Cantonese, Dutch, German, Greek, Gujarati, Hindi, Italian, Korean, Mandarin, Persian, Polish, Portuguese, Punjabi, Russian, Somali, Spanish, Tagalog, Tamil, Urdu and Vietnamese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Ontario" title="Economy of Ontario">Economy of Ontario</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yellow_Boat_-_Hamilton_Harbour_-_Hamilton_Flickr_Meet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1017" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Yellow_Boat_-_Hamilton_Harbour_-_Hamilton_Flickr_Meet.jpg/220px-Yellow_Boat_-_Hamilton_Harbour_-_Hamilton_Flickr_Meet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Yellow_Boat_-_Hamilton_Harbour_-_Hamilton_Flickr_Meet.jpg/330px-Yellow_Boat_-_Hamilton_Harbour_-_Hamilton_Flickr_Meet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Yellow_Boat_-_Hamilton_Harbour_-_Hamilton_Flickr_Meet.jpg/440px-Yellow_Boat_-_Hamilton_Harbour_-_Hamilton_Flickr_Meet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yellow_Boat_-_Hamilton_Harbour_-_Hamilton_Flickr_Meet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ship in <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Harbour" title="Hamilton Harbour">Hamilton Harbour</a>. The manufacturing sector is a major employer in Ontario.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ontario is Canada's leading <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> province, accounting for 52% of the total national manufacturing shipments in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Ontario's largest trading partner is the American state of <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>. As of April 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Moody%27s_Corporation" title="Moody's Corporation">Moody's</a> bond-rating agency rated <a href="/wiki/Canadian_public_debt" title="Canadian public debt">Ontario debt</a> at AA2/stable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> while S&amp;P rated it AA-.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Dominion Bond Rating Service rated it AA(low) in January 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Long known as a bastion of Canadian manufacturing and financial solvency, Ontario's public <a href="/wiki/Debt-to-GDP_ratio" title="Debt-to-GDP ratio">debt-to-GDP ratio</a> is projected to be 38.6% in <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> 2016–2017, compared to 26% in 2007–2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ontario's rivers make it rich in hydroelectric energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In 2009, <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Power_Generation" title="Ontario Power Generation">Ontario Power Generation</a> generated 70 percent of the electricity of the province, of which 51 percent is <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear</a>, 39% is <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric</a> and 10% is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel" title="Fossil-fuel">fossil-fuel</a> derived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> By 2025, nuclear power is projected to supply 42%, while fossil-fuel-derived generation is projected to decrease slightly over the next 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gm2dec_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-gm2dec-55">[55]</a></sup> Much of the newer power generation coming online in the last few years is natural gas or combined-cycle natural gas plants. OPG is not, however, responsible for the transmission of power, which is under the control of <a href="/wiki/Hydro_One" title="Hydro One">Hydro One</a>. Despite its diverse range of power options, problems related to increasing consumption, lack of energy efficiency and aging nuclear reactors, Ontario has been forced in recent years to purchase power from its neighbours Quebec and Michigan to supplement its power needs during peak consumption periods. Ontario's basic domestic rate in 2010 was 11.17 cents per kWH; by contrast. Quebec's was 6.81.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In December 2013, the government projected a 42 percent hike by 2018, and 68 percent by 2033.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gm2dec_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-gm2dec-55">[55]</a></sup> Industrial rates are projected to rise by 33% by 2018, and 55% in 2033.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gm2dec_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-gm2dec-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>An abundance of <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a>, excellent transportation links to the American heartland and the inland Great Lakes making ocean access possible via <a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ships</a>, have all contributed to making <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> the principal <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">industry</a> of the province, found mainly in the Golden Horseshoe region, which is the largest industrialized area in Canada, the southern end of the region being part of the North American <a href="/wiki/Rust_Belt" title="Rust Belt">Rust Belt</a>. Important products include <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle" title="Motor vehicle">motor vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, food, electrical appliances, machinery, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemicals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>.</p>
<p>Ontario surpassed Michigan in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">car</a> production, assembling 2.696 million vehicles in 2004. Ontario has <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a> plants in <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor</a> and Bramalea, two <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">GM</a> plants in Oshawa and one in Ingersoll, a <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a> assembly plant in Alliston, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford" title="Ford">Ford</a> plants in Oakville and St. Thomas and <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a> assembly plants in Cambridge and Woodstock. However, as a result of steeply declining sales, in 2005, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> announced massive layoffs at production facilities across North America including two large GM plants in <a href="/wiki/Oshawa" title="Oshawa">Oshawa</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Powertrain" title="Powertrain">drive train</a> facility in <a href="/wiki/St._Catharines" title="St. Catharines">St. Catharines</a> resulting in 8,000 job losses in Ontario alone. In 2006, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a> announced between 25,000 and 30,000 layoffs phased until 2012; Ontario was spared the worst, but job losses were announced for the <a href="/wiki/St._Thomas,_Ontario" title="St. Thomas, Ontario">St Thomas</a> facility and the <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Casting" title="Windsor Casting">Windsor Casting</a> plant. However, these losses will be offset by Ford's recent announcement of a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid vehicle</a> facility slated to begin production in 2007 at its <a href="/wiki/Oakville,_Ontario" title="Oakville, Ontario">Oakville</a> plant and GM's re-introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Camaro" title="Chevrolet Camaro">Camaro</a> which will be produced in Oshawa. On December 4, 2008 <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a> announced the grand opening of the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_RAV4" title="Toyota RAV4">RAV4</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Woodstock,_Ontario" title="Woodstock, Ontario">Woodstock</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a> also has plans to add an engine plant at its facility in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alliston,_Ontario" title="Alliston, Ontario">Alliston</a>. Despite these new plants coming online, Ontario has not yet fully recovered following massive layoffs caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932010" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2010">global recession</a>; its unemployment rate was 7.3% in May 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> compared to 8.7 percent in January 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and approximately 6% in 2007. In September 2013, the Ontario government committed CAD$70.9 million to the Ford plant in <a href="/wiki/Oakville,_Ontario" title="Oakville, Ontario">Oakville</a>, while the federal government committed CAD$71.1mn, to secure 2,800 jobs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The province has lost 300,000 manufacturing jobs in the decade from 2003, and the Bank of Canada noted that "while the energy and mining industries have benefitted from these movements, the pressure on the manufacturing sector has intensified, since many firms in this sector were already dealing with growing competition from low-cost economies such as China."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ontario's steel industry was once centred on <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ontario" title="Hamilton, Ontario">Hamilton</a>. Hamilton harbour, which can be seen as one drives the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QEW" title="QEW">QEW</a> Skyway bridge, is an industrial wasteland; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Steel" title="US Steel">US Steel</a>-owned <a href="/wiki/Stelco" title="Stelco">Stelco</a> announced in the autumn of 2013 that it would close in 2014, with the loss of 875 jobs. The move flummoxed a union representative, who seemed puzzled why a plant with capacity of 2 million tons per annum would be shut while Canada imported 8 million tons of steel the year before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algoma_Steel" title="Algoma Steel">Algoma Steel</a> still maintains a plant in <a href="/wiki/Sault_Ste._Marie,_Ontario" title="Sault Ste. Marie, Ontario">Sault Ste Marie</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toronto-view-from-cn-tower.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Toronto-view-from-cn-tower.jpg/220px-Toronto-view-from-cn-tower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Toronto-view-from-cn-tower.jpg/330px-Toronto-view-from-cn-tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Toronto-view-from-cn-tower.jpg/440px-Toronto-view-from-cn-tower.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toronto-view-from-cn-tower.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of Toronto's <a href="/wiki/Financial_District,_Toronto" title="Financial District, Toronto">Financial District</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, the capital of Ontario, is the centre of Canada's <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial services</a> and banking industry. Neighbouring cities are home to product distribution, IT centres, and various manufacturing industries. Canada's Federal Government is the largest single employer in the <a href="/wiki/National_Capital_Region_(Canada)" title="National Capital Region (Canada)">National Capital Region</a>, which centres on the border cities of Ontario's Ottawa and Quebec's <a href="/wiki/Gatineau" title="Gatineau">Gatineau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parliament_Hill_Front_Entrance.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1037" data-file-width="1382" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Parliament_Hill_Front_Entrance.jpg/220px-Parliament_Hill_Front_Entrance.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Parliament_Hill_Front_Entrance.jpg/330px-Parliament_Hill_Front_Entrance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Parliament_Hill_Front_Entrance.jpg/440px-Parliament_Hill_Front_Entrance.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parliament_Hill_Front_Entrance.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Parliament_Hill" title="Parliament Hill">Parliament Hill</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, home of the federal government. Canada's Federal Government is the largest single employer in the <a href="/wiki/National_Capital_Region_(Canada)" title="National Capital Region (Canada)">National Capital Region</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">information technology</a> sector is important, particularly in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley_North" title="Silicon Valley North">Silicon Valley North</a></i> section of <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Municipality_of_Waterloo" title="Regional Municipality of Waterloo">Waterloo Region</a>, where the world headquarters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Research_in_Motion" title="Research in Motion">Research in Motion</a> (the developers of the <a href="/wiki/BlackBerry" title="BlackBerry">BlackBerry</a> smartphone) is located. BlackBerry once provided more than 19 percent of the local jobs and employed more than 13% of the entire local population<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> before it supplied 9,500 layoffs in 2013. <a href="/wiki/OpenText" title="OpenText">OpenText</a> and <a href="/wiki/ATS_Automation_Tooling_Systems" title="ATS Automation Tooling Systems">ATS Automation Tooling Systems</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Ontario" title="Cambridge, Ontario">Cambridge</a> make their homes in the area too. <a href="/wiki/Mike_Lazaridis" title="Mike Lazaridis">Mike Lazaridis</a>, one of the founders of RIM, founded in 1999 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perimeter_Institute" title="Perimeter Institute">Perimeter Institute</a>, then in 2002 the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Quantum_Computing" title="Institute for Quantum Computing">Institute for Quantum Computing</a>, then in 2013 Quantum Valley Investments, to plow a portion of the benefits of RIM back into research and development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, the section of Highway 401 between Toronto and Waterloo became the world's second-largest innovation corridor after California's <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>, employing nearly 280,000 tech workers from around the world and containing over 60% of Canada's <a href="/wiki/High_tech" title="High tech">high tech</a> industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ontario" title="Hamilton, Ontario">Hamilton</a> is the largest steel manufacturing city in Canada followed closely by <a href="/wiki/Sault_Ste._Marie,_Ontario" title="Sault Ste. Marie, Ontario">Sault Ste. Marie</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sarnia" title="Sarnia">Sarnia</a> is the centre for <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemical</a> production. <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">Construction</a> employed more than 6.5% of the province's work force in June 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">Mining</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forest products</a> industry, notably <a href="/wiki/Pulp_and_paper_industry_in_Canada" title="Pulp and paper industry in Canada">pulp and paper</a>, are vital to the economy of Northern Ontario. There has been controversy over the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ontario_Ring_of_Fire" title="Northern Ontario Ring of Fire">Ring of Fire</a> mineral deposit, and whether the province can afford to spend CAD$2.25 billion on a road from the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Canada_Highway" title="Trans-Canada Highway">Trans-Canada Highway</a> near <a href="/wiki/Kenora" title="Kenora">Kenora</a> to the deposit, currently valued at CAD$60 billions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tourism contributes heavily to the economy of Central Ontario, peaking during the summer months owing to the abundance of <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> recreation and wilderness found there in reasonable proximity to the major urban centres. At other times of the year, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skiing" title="Skiing">skiing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snowmobile" title="Snowmobile">snowmobiling</a> are popular. This region has some of the most vibrant fall colour displays anywhere on the continent, and tours directed at overseas visitors are organized to see them. Tourism also plays a key role in border cities with large casinos, among them Windsor, <a href="/wiki/Cornwall,_Ontario" title="Cornwall, Ontario">Cornwall</a>, Sarnia and <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls,_Ontario" title="Niagara Falls, Ontario">Niagara Falls</a>, the latter of which attracts millions of US and other international visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Niagara_fruit_1914.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="537" data-file-width="819" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Niagara_fruit_1914.jpg/220px-Niagara_fruit_1914.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Niagara_fruit_1914.jpg/330px-Niagara_fruit_1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Niagara_fruit_1914.jpg/440px-Niagara_fruit_1914.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Niagara_fruit_1914.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fruit from the Niagara region for distribution, ca. 1914</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eatonville.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="715" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Eatonville.jpg/220px-Eatonville.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Eatonville.jpg/330px-Eatonville.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Eatonville.jpg/440px-Eatonville.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eatonville.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eaton Farm in <a href="/wiki/Eatonville,_Toronto" title="Eatonville, Toronto">Eatonville</a> provided poultry, vegetables, dairy and meat products for <a href="/wiki/Eaton%27s" title="Eaton's">Eaton's</a> department stores until the early 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canadian_Jewish_Farm_School,_Georgetown,_Ontario_(1929).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="760" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Canadian_Jewish_Farm_School%2C_Georgetown%2C_Ontario_%281929%29.jpg/220px-Canadian_Jewish_Farm_School%2C_Georgetown%2C_Ontario_%281929%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Canadian_Jewish_Farm_School%2C_Georgetown%2C_Ontario_%281929%29.jpg/330px-Canadian_Jewish_Farm_School%2C_Georgetown%2C_Ontario_%281929%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Canadian_Jewish_Farm_School%2C_Georgetown%2C_Ontario_%281929%29.jpg/440px-Canadian_Jewish_Farm_School%2C_Georgetown%2C_Ontario_%281929%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canadian_Jewish_Farm_School,_Georgetown,_Ontario_(1929).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Canadian Jewish Farm School in <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Ontario" title="Georgetown, Ontario">Georgetown, Ontario</a> was established in 1927 and served as a training school for Polish war orphans brought to Canada after the First World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Once the dominant industry, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> occupies a small percentage of the population. However, much of the land in southern Ontario is given over to agriculture. As the following table shows, while the number of individual farms has steadily decreased and their overall size has shrunk at a lower rate, greater mechanization has supported increased supply to satisfy the ever-increasing demands of a growing population base; this has also meant a gradual increase in the total amount of land used for growing crops.</p>

</center>
<p><br/>
Common types of farms reported in the 2001 census include those for cattle, small grains and <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a>. The fruit- and grape-growing industry is located primarily on the <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Peninsula" title="Niagara Peninsula">Niagara Peninsula</a> and along Lake Erie, where <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> farms are also situated. Market vegetables grow in the rich soils of the <a href="/wiki/Holland_Marsh" title="Holland Marsh">Holland Marsh</a> near <a href="/wiki/Newmarket,_Ontario" title="Newmarket, Ontario">Newmarket</a>. The area near <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor</a> is also very fertile. The Heinz plant in <a href="/wiki/Leamington,_Ontario" title="Leamington, Ontario">Leamington</a> was taken over in these autumn of 2013 by <a href="/wiki/Warren_Buffett" title="Warren Buffett">Warren Buffett</a> and a Brazilian partner, following which it put 740 people out of work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Government subsidies followed shortly; Premier <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Wynne" title="Kathleen Wynne">Kathleen Wynne</a> offered CAD$200,000 to cushion the blow, and promised that another processed-food operator would soon be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> On December 10, 2013, <a href="/wiki/Kellogg%27s" title="Kellogg's">Kellogg's</a> announced layoffs for more than 509 workers at a cereal manufacture plant in <a href="/wiki/London,_Ontario" title="London, Ontario">London</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gmkellogs_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-gmkellogs-76">[76]</a></sup> Kellogg's plans to relocate jobs to Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gmkellogs_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-gmkellogs-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The area defined as the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Belt" title="Corn Belt">Corn Belt</a> covers much of the southwestern area of the province, extending as far north as close to Goderich, but corn and soy are grown throughout the southern portion of the province. Apple orchards are a common sight along the southern shore of <a href="/wiki/Nottawasaga_Bay" title="Nottawasaga Bay">Nottawasaga Bay</a> (part of Georgian Bay) near Collingwood and along the northern shore of Lake Ontario near Cobourg. Tobacco production, centred in <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_County,_Ontario" title="Norfolk County, Ontario">Norfolk County</a>, has decreased, allowing an increase in alternative crops such as <a href="/wiki/Corylus_avellana" title="Corylus avellana">hazelnuts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ginseng" title="Ginseng">ginseng</a>. The Ontario origins of <a href="/wiki/Massey_Ferguson" title="Massey Ferguson">Massey Ferguson</a>, once one of the largest <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_machinery" title="Agricultural machinery">farm-implement</a> manufacturers in the world, indicate the importance agriculture once<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> had to the Canadian economy.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Southern_Ontario" title="Southern Ontario">Southern Ontario</a>'s limited supply of agricultural land is going out of production at an increasing rate. <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">Urban sprawl</a> and farmland severances contribute to the loss of thousands of acres of productive agricultural land in Ontario each year. Over 2,000 farms and 150,000 acres (61,000 ha) of farmland in the GTA alone were lost to production in the two decades between 1976 and 1996. This loss represented approximately 18%". of Ontario's Class 1 farmland being converted to urban purposes. In addition, increasing rural <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Severance_(disambiguation)" title="Severance (disambiguation)">severances</a> provide ever-greater interference with agricultural production.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_Canada" title="Energy policy of Canada">Energy policy of Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Canada" title="Renewable energy in Canada">Renewable energy in Canada</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Smart_grid" title="Smart grid">Smart grid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="1000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/220px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/330px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/440px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDU" title="CANDU">CANDU</a> <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Bruce Nuclear Generating Station">Bruce Nuclear Generating Station</a> on Lake Huron is the largest <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> plant in the world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Green Energy and Green Economy Act, 2009 (GEA), takes a two-pronged approach to commercializing renewable energy:</p>
<ol>
<li>bringing more <a href="/wiki/Renewable_resource" title="Renewable resource">renewable energy sources</a> to the province</li>
<li>adopting more <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy-efficiency</a> measures to help <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">conserve energy</a></li>
</ol>
<p>The bill envisaged appointing a Renewable Energy Facilitator to provide "one-window" assistance and support to project developers to facilitate project approvals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renewableenergyworld1_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-renewableenergyworld1-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The approvals process for transmission projects would also be <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/streamline" title="wikt:streamline">streamlined</a> and (for the first time in Ontario) the bill would enact standards for renewable energy projects. Homeowners would have access to incentives to develop small-scale renewables such as low- or no-interest <a href="/wiki/Loan" title="Loan">loans</a> to finance the capital cost of renewable energy generating facilities like solar panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renewableenergyworld1_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-renewableenergyworld1-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ontario is home to <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls" title="Niagara Falls">Niagara Falls</a>, which supplies a large amount of electricity to the province. The <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Bruce Nuclear Generating Station">Bruce Nuclear Generating Station</a>, the largest <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> plant in the world, is also in Ontario and uses 8 <a href="/wiki/CANDU_reactor" title="CANDU reactor">CANDU reactors</a> to generate electricity for the province.</p>
<h2><span id="Government,_law_and_politics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Government.2C_law_and_politics">Government, law and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Government, law and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_in_Ontario" title="Monarchy in Ontario">Monarchy in Ontario</a> and <a href="/wiki/Executive_Council_of_Ontario" title="Executive Council of Ontario">Executive Council of Ontario</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Queen%27s_Park.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Queen%27s_Park.jpg/220px-Queen%27s_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Queen%27s_Park.jpg/330px-Queen%27s_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Queen%27s_Park.jpg/440px-Queen%27s_Park.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Queen%27s_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_Ontario" title="Legislative Assembly of Ontario">Ontario Legislature</a> at <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Park_(Toronto)" title="Queen's Park (Toronto)">Queen's Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ontario-wordmark.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="91" data-file-width="306" height="51" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/55/Ontario-wordmark.svg/170px-Ontario-wordmark.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/55/Ontario-wordmark.svg/255px-Ontario-wordmark.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/55/Ontario-wordmark.svg/340px-Ontario-wordmark.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ontario-wordmark.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The previous wordmark of the Government of Ontario, which was in use from the late-1960s until 2007 (apart from the lettering used here).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i>British North America Act 1867</i> section 69 stipulated "There shall be a Legislature for Ontario consisting of the Lieutenant Governor and of One House, styled the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_Ontario" title="Legislative Assembly of Ontario">Legislative Assembly of Ontario</a>." The assembly has 107 seats representing <a href="/wiki/Electoral_district_(Canada)" title="Electoral district (Canada)">ridings</a> elected in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plurality_voting_system" title="Plurality voting system">first-past-the-post</a> system across the province.</p>
<p>The legislative buildings at <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Park_(Toronto)" title="Queen's Park (Toronto)">Queen's Park</a> are the seat of government. Following the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a>, the leader of the party holding the most seats in the assembly is known as the "Premier and President of the Council" (Executive Council Act R.S.O. 1990). The Premier chooses the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_(government)" title="Cabinet (government)">cabinet</a> or <a href="/wiki/Executive_Council_of_Ontario" title="Executive Council of Ontario">Executive Council</a> whose members are deemed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministers_of_the_Crown" title="Ministers of the Crown">ministers of the Crown</a>.</p>
<p>Although the <i>Legislative Assembly Act (R.S.O. 1990)</i> refers to "members of the assembly", the legislators are now commonly called MPPs (<a href="/wiki/Member_of_Provincial_Parliament_(Ontario)" title="Member of Provincial Parliament (Ontario)">Members of the Provincial Parliament</a>) in English and <i>députés de l'Assemblée législative</i> in French, but they have also been called MLAs (<a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Legislative_Assembly" title="Member of the Legislative Assembly">Members of the Legislative Assembly</a>), and both are acceptable. The title of Prime Minister of Ontario, correct in French (<i>le Premier ministre</i>), is permissible in English but now generally avoided in favour of the title "Premier" to avoid confusion with the Prime Minister of Canada.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ontario has grown, from its roots in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">Upper Canada</a>, into a modern jurisdiction. The old titles of the chief law officers, the Attorney-General and the Solicitor-General, remain in use. They both are responsible to the Legislature. The Attorney-General drafts the laws and is responsible for criminal prosecutions and the administration of justice, while the Solicitor-General is responsible for law enforcement and the police services of the province.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Ontario" title="Politics of Ontario">Politics of Ontario</a></div>
<p>Ontario has numerous political parties which run for election. The three main parties are the centre-left <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Liberal_Party" title="Ontario Liberal Party">Ontario Liberal Party</a>, the centre-right <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Conservative_Party_of_Ontario" title="Progressive Conservative Party of Ontario">Progressive Conservative Party of Ontario</a>, and the social democratic <a href="/wiki/Ontario_New_Democratic_Party" title="Ontario New Democratic Party">Ontario New Democratic Party</a> (NDP). Each of the three parties has received a majority mandate during a provincial election since <a href="/wiki/Ontario_general_election,_1990" title="Ontario general election, 1990">1990</a>.</p>
<p>Ontario is led by the <a href="/wiki/Majority_government" title="Majority government">majority government</a> of Premier <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Wynne" title="Kathleen Wynne">Kathleen Wynne</a>, a Liberal. Since gaining power under former Premier <a href="/wiki/Dalton_McGuinty" title="Dalton McGuinty">Dalton McGuinty</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ontario_general_election,_2003" title="Ontario general election, 2003">2003</a>, the Ontario Liberals have been re-elected three times: in the <a href="/wiki/Ontario_general_election,_2007" title="Ontario general election, 2007">2007</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ontario_general_election,_2011" title="Ontario general election, 2011">2011</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ontario_general_election,_2014" title="Ontario general election, 2014">2014</a> general elections.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_federal_election,_2011" title="Canadian federal election, 2011">2011 federal election</a> in Ontario the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Conservative Party of Canada">Conservatives</a> were elected in 73 ridings, the <a href="/wiki/New_Democratic_Party" title="New Democratic Party">NDP</a> in 22, and the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Canada" title="Liberal Party of Canada">Liberals</a> in 11. The <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_Canada" title="Green Party of Canada">Green Party</a> did not win a seat in Ontario, but <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Hyer" title="Bruce Hyer">Bruce Hyer</a> (MP for <a href="/wiki/Thunder_Bay%E2%80%94Superior_North" title="Thunder Bay—Superior North">Thunder Bay—Superior North</a>) <a href="/wiki/Crossing_the_floor#Changing_parties" title="Crossing the floor">crossed the floor</a> from the NDP and sat as a Green Party member from 2013 until the dissolution of Parliament for the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_federal_election,_2015" title="Canadian federal election, 2015">2015 federal election</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_areas">Urban areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Urban areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horseshoe" title="Golden Horseshoe">Golden Horseshoe</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Capital_Region_(Canada)" title="National Capital Region (Canada)">National Capital Region (Canada)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Detroit%E2%80%93Windsor" title="Detroit–Windsor">Detroit–Windsor</a></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Ontario" title="Education in Ontario">Education in Ontario</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, education falls under provincial jurisdiction. Publicly funded elementary and secondary schools are administered by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_(Ontario)" title="Ministry of Education (Ontario)">Ontario Ministry of Education</a>, while colleges and universities are administered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Training,_Colleges_and_Universities" title="Ministry of Training, Colleges and Universities">Ontario Ministry of Training, Colleges and Universities</a>. The current Minister of Education is <a href="/wiki/Mitzie_Hunter" title="Mitzie Hunter">Mitzie Hunter</a>, and the current minister of Training, Colleges and Universities is <a href="/wiki/Reza_Moridi" title="Reza Moridi">Reza Moridi</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_education">Higher education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Higher education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_Ontario" title="Higher education in Ontario">Higher education in Ontario</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_colleges_in_Ontario" title="List of colleges in Ontario">List of colleges in Ontario</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Ontario" title="List of universities in Ontario">List of universities in Ontario</a></div>
<p>Higher education in Ontario includes postsecondary education and skills training regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_(Ontario)" title="Ministry of Education (Ontario)">Ministry of Training, Colleges, and Universities</a> and provided by universities, colleges of applied arts and technology, and private career colleges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTCU-Role_of_the_ministry_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTCU-Role_of_the_ministry-80">[80]</a></sup> The current minister is <a href="/wiki/Reza_Moridi" title="Reza Moridi">Reza Moridi</a>. The ministry administers laws covering 22 public universities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTCU-Find_a_university_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTCU-Find_a_university-81">[81]</a></sup> 24 public colleges (21 Colleges of Applied Arts and Technology (CAATs) and three Institutes of Technology and Advanced Learning (ITALs)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTCU-Find_a_college_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTCU-Find_a_college-82">[82]</a></sup> 17 privately funded religious universities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTCU-Privately_funded_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTCU-Privately_funded-83">[83]</a></sup> and over 500 private career colleges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTCU-Private_career_colleges_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTCU-Private_career_colleges-84">[84]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_constitution" title="Canadian constitution">Canadian constitution</a> provides each province with the responsibility for <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a> and there is no corresponding national federal ministry of higher education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDN_Constitution_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDN_Constitution-85">[85]</a></sup> Within <a href="/wiki/Canadian_federalism" title="Canadian federalism">Canadian federalism</a> the division of responsibilities and taxing powers between the Ontario and Canadian governments creates the need for co-operation to fund and deliver higher education to students. Each higher education system aims to improve participation, access, and mobility for students. There are two central organizations that assist with the process of applying to Ontario universities and colleges: the <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Universities%27_Application_Centre" title="Ontario Universities' Application Centre">Ontario Universities' Application Centre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ontario_College_Application_Service" title="Ontario College Application Service">Ontario College Application Service</a>. While application services are centralized, admission and selection processes vary and are the purview of each institution independently. Admission to many Ontario postsecondary institutions can be highly competitive. Upon admission, students may get involved with regional student representation with the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Federation_of_Students" title="Canadian Federation of Students">Canadian Federation of Students</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Alliance_of_Student_Associations" title="Canadian Alliance of Student Associations">Canadian Alliance of Student Associations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Undergraduate_Student_Alliance" title="Ontario Undergraduate Student Alliance">Ontario Undergraduate Student Alliance</a>, or through the <a href="/wiki/College_Student_Alliance" title="College Student Alliance">College Student Alliance</a> in Ontario.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Songs_and_slogans">Songs and slogans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Songs and slogans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1973 the first slogan to appear on <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plates_of_Ontario" title="Vehicle registration plates of Ontario">licence plates in Ontario</a> was "Keep It Beautiful". This was replaced by "Yours to Discover" in 1982,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> apparently inspired by a tourism slogan, "Discover Ontario", dating back to 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Plates with the French equivalent, "Tant à découvrir", were made available to the public beginning in May 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> (From 1988 to 1990,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> "Ontario Incredible"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> gave "Yours to Discover" a brief respite.)</p>
<p>In 2007, a new song replaced "A Place to Stand" after four decades. "There's No Place Like This" is featured in current <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_advertisement" title="Television advertisement">television advertising</a>, performed by Ontario artists including <a href="/wiki/Molly_Johnson" title="Molly Johnson">Molly Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Byrne" title="Brian Byrne">Brian Byrne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keshia_Chant%C3%A9" title="Keshia Chanté">Keshia Chanté</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Tomi_Swick" title="Tomi Swick">Tomi Swick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arkells" title="Arkells">Arkells</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_residents">Notable residents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notable residents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_from_Ontario" title="List of people from Ontario">List of people from Ontario</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_sports">Professional sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Professional sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The province has professional sports teams in <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canadian_football" title="Canadian football">Canadian football</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lacrosse" title="Lacrosse">lacrosse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">rugby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">soccer</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Transportation routes in Ontario evolved from early waterway travel and First Nations paths followed by European explorers. Ontario has two major east-west routes, both starting from Montreal in the neighbouring province of Quebec. The northerly route, which was a major <a href="/wiki/Fur_trade" title="Fur trade">fur trade</a> route, travels west from Montreal along the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_River" title="Ottawa River">Ottawa River</a>, then continues northwestward towards Manitoba. Major cities on or near the route include Ottawa, North Bay, Sudbury, <a href="/wiki/Sault_Ste._Marie,_Ontario" title="Sault Ste. Marie, Ontario">Sault Ste. Marie</a>, and Thunder Bay. The southerly route, which was driven by growth in settlements originated by the United Empire Loyalists and later other <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">European immigrants</a>, travels southwest from Montreal along the St. Lawrence River, Lake Ontario, and Lake Erie before entering the United States in Michigan. Major cities on or near the route include Kingston, Belleville, Peterborough, Oshawa, Toronto, Mississauga, <a href="/wiki/Regional_Municipality_of_Waterloo" title="Regional Municipality of Waterloo">Kitchener-Waterloo</a>, Hamilton, London, Sarnia, and Windsor. This route was also heavily used by immigrants to the Midwestern US particularly in the late 19th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">Roads</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Roads">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roads_in_Ontario" title="Roads in Ontario">Roads in Ontario</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/400-series_highways_(Ontario)" title="400-series highways (Ontario)">400-Series Highways</a> make up the primary vehicular network in the south of province, and they connect to numerous border crossings with the US, the busiest being the <a href="/wiki/Detroit%E2%80%93Windsor_Tunnel" title="Detroit–Windsor Tunnel">Detroit–Windsor Tunnel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ambassador_Bridge" title="Ambassador Bridge">Ambassador Bridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Water_Bridge" title="Blue Water Bridge">Blue Water Bridge</a> (via <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Highway_402" title="Ontario Highway 402">Highway 402</a>). Some of the primary highways along the southern route are Highway 401, <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Highway_417" title="Ontario Highway 417">Highway 417</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Highway_400" title="Ontario Highway 400">Highway 400</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ontario_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ontario-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Highway_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Highway-93">[93]</a></sup> while other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_provincial_highways_in_Ontario" title="List of provincial highways in Ontario">provincial highways</a> and regional roads inter-connect the remainder of the province.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waterways">Waterways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Waterways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_Seaway" title="Saint Lawrence Seaway">Saint Lawrence Seaway</a>, which extends across most of the southern portion of the province and connects to the Atlantic Ocean, is the primary <a href="/wiki/Water_transportation" title="Water transportation">water transportation</a> route for cargo, particularly <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a> and grain. In the past, the Great Lakes and St. Lawrence River were also a major passenger transportation route, but over the past half century passenger travel has been reduced to ferry services and sightseeing cruises.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Railways">Railways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Railways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Via_Rail" title="Via Rail">Via Rail</a> operates the inter-regional passenger train service on the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City%E2%80%93Windsor_Corridor" title="Quebec City–Windsor Corridor">Quebec City–Windsor Corridor</a>, along with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Canadian" title="The Canadian">The Canadian</a></i>, a transcontinental rail service from Southern Ontario to <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury%E2%80%93White_River_train" title="Sudbury–White River train">Sudbury–White River train</a>. Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a> rail connects Ontario with key New York cities including <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Northland_Railway" title="Ontario Northland Railway">Ontario Northland</a> provides rail service to destinations as far north as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moosonee,_Ontario" title="Moosonee, Ontario">Moosonee</a> near <a href="/wiki/James_Bay" title="James Bay">James Bay</a>, connecting them with the south.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freight_train" title="Freight train">Freight rail</a> is dominated by the founding cross-country <a href="/wiki/Canadian_National_Railway" title="Canadian National Railway">Canadian National Railway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Pacific_Railway" title="Canadian Pacific Railway">CP Rail</a> companies, which during the 1990s sold many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short-line_railroad" title="Short-line railroad">short rail lines</a> from their vast network to private companies operating mostly in the south.</p>
<p>Regional <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail" title="Commuter rail">commuter rail</a> is limited to the provincially owned <a href="/wiki/GO_Transit" title="GO Transit">GO Transit</a>, and serves a train-bus network spanning the Golden Horseshoe region.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Transit_Commission" title="Toronto Transit Commission">Toronto Transit Commission</a> operates the province's only <a href="/wiki/Toronto_subway" title="Toronto subway">subway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toronto_streetcar_system" title="Toronto streetcar system">streetcar</a> system, one of the busiest in North America. <a href="/wiki/OC_Transpo" title="OC Transpo">OC Transpo</a> operates, in addition to bus service, Ontario's only <a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">light rail</a> transit line, the <a href="/wiki/O-Train" title="O-Train">O-Train</a> in Ottawa.</p>
<p>A light-rail metro called the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_Line" title="Confederation Line">Confederation Line</a> is under construction in <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>. It will have 13 stations on 12.5 km (7.8 mi) and part of it will run under the city's <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Ottawa" title="Downtown Ottawa">Downtown</a> and feature three underground stations. In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Ion_rapid_transit" title="Ion rapid transit">Ion light rail and bus rapid transit system</a> is under construction in the province's <a href="/wiki/Regional_Municipality_of_Waterloo" title="Regional Municipality of Waterloo">Waterloo region</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1008px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ontario_Northland_train_crosses_the_Missinaibi_River_at_Mattice-Val_C%C3%B4t%C3%A9,_Ontario.jpg" title="Ontario Northland freight train crossing the Missinaibi River at Mattice-Val Côté in Northern Ontario"><img alt="Ontario Northland freight train crossing the Missinaibi River at Mattice-Val Côté in Northern Ontario" data-file-height="278" data-file-width="1200" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ontario_Northland_train_crosses_the_Missinaibi_River_at_Mattice-Val_C%C3%B4t%C3%A9%2C_Ontario.jpg/1000px-Ontario_Northland_train_crosses_the_Missinaibi_River_at_Mattice-Val_C%C3%B4t%C3%A9%2C_Ontario.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Ontario_Northland_train_crosses_the_Missinaibi_River_at_Mattice-Val_C%C3%B4t%C3%A9%2C_Ontario.jpg 1.5x" width="1000"/></a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_travel">Air travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontario&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Air travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Important airports in the province include <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Pearson_International_Airport" title="Toronto Pearson International Airport">Toronto Pearson International Airport</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_busiest_airports_in_Canada" title="List of the busiest airports in Canada">busiest airport in Canada</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-move_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-move-94">[94]</a></sup> handling over 41 million passengers in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15DecPax_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-15DecPax-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Macdonald%E2%80%93Cartier_International_Airport" title="Ottawa Macdonald–Cartier International Airport">Ottawa Macdonald–Cartier International Airport</a> is Ontario's second largest airport. Toronto/Pearson and Ottawa/Macdonald-Cartier form two of the three points in Canada's busiest set of air routes (the third point being <a href="/wiki/Montr%C3%A9al%E2%80%93Pierre_Elliott_Trudeau_International_Airport" title="Montréal–Pierre Elliott Trudeau International Airport">Montréal–Pierre Elliott Trudeau International Airport</a>).</p>
<p>Most Ontario cities have regional airports, many of which have scheduled commuter flights from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_Canada_Jazz" title="Air Canada Jazz">Air Canada Jazz</a> or smaller airlines and charter companies – flights from the mid-size cities such as Thunder Bay, Sault Ste. Marie, Sudbury, North Bay, Timmins, Windsor, London, and Kingston feed directly into larger airports in Toronto and Ottawa. <a href="/wiki/Bearskin_Airlines" title="Bearskin Airlines">Bearskin Airlines</a> also runs flights along the northerly east-west route, connecting Ottawa, North Bay, Sudbury, Sault Ste. Marie, Kitchener and Thunder Bay directly.</p>
<p>Isolated towns and settlements in the northern areas of the province rely partly or entirely on <a href="/wiki/Airline" title="Airline">air service</a> for travel, goods, and even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_ambulance" title="Air ambulance">ambulance</a> services (<a href="/wiki/Medical_evacuation" title="Medical evacuation">MEDIVAC</a>), since much of the far northern area of the province cannot be reached by road or rail.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 227.33333333333px">
<div style="width: 227.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 225.33333333333px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:401_Gridlock.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1600" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/401_Gridlock.jpg/338px-401_Gridlock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/401_Gridlock.jpg/508px-401_Gridlock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/401_Gridlock.jpg/676px-401_Gridlock.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Ontario_Highway_401" title="Ontario Highway 401">Highway 401</a> is the busiest highway in North America and among the busiest highways in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ontario_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ontario-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Highway_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Highway-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 189.33333333333px">
<div style="width: 189.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 187.33333333333px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lakeshore_West_GO_Train_Westbound.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1652" data-file-width="2064" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lakeshore_West_GO_Train_Westbound.jpg/281px-Lakeshore_West_GO_Train_Westbound.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lakeshore_West_GO_Train_Westbound.jpg/422px-Lakeshore_West_GO_Train_Westbound.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lakeshore_West_GO_Train_Westbound.jpg/562px-Lakeshore_West_GO_Train_Westbound.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A <a href="/wiki/GO_Transit" title="GO Transit">GO Transit</a> commuter train.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px">
<div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toronto_Airport.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1782" data-file-width="2376" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Toronto_Airport.jpg/300px-Toronto_Airport.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Toronto_Airport.jpg/451px-Toronto_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Toronto_Airport.jpg/600px-Toronto_Airport.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Air_Canada" title="Air Canada">Air Canada</a> is the largest airline operating in Ontario. Its largest hub is at <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Pearson_International_Airport" title="Toronto Pearson International Airport">Pearson International Airport</a> in Mississauga.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px">
<div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:O_Train_over_Rideau_by_Wilder.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/O_Train_over_Rideau_by_Wilder.JPG/300px-O_Train_over_Rideau_by_Wilder.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/O_Train_over_Rideau_by_Wilder.JPG/451px-O_Train_over_Rideau_by_Wilder.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/O_Train_over_Rideau_by_Wilder.JPG/600px-O_Train_over_Rideau_by_Wilder.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A light rail <a href="/wiki/O-Train" title="O-Train">O-Train</a> crossing the Rideau River on the <a href="/wiki/Trillium_Line" title="Trillium Line">Trillium Line</a>.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
